<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Iridium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">Indium</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Iridium_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Iridium (disambiguation)">Iridium (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 77</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Iridium,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">77</span></sub>Ir</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Iridium-2.jpg" class="image" title="Pieces of pure iridium"><img alt="Pieces of pure iridium" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Iridium-2.jpg/220px-Iridium-2.jpg" width="220" height="190" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Iridium-2.jpg/330px-Iridium-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Iridium-2.jpg/440px-Iridium-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="528" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ffc0c0">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">i-<span style="font-size:90%">RID</span>-ee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
silvery white</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;">&#x20;(<i>A</i><sub>r,&#160;standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_iridium" title="Template:Infobox iridium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_iridium" title="Template talk:Infobox iridium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_iridium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Iridium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Ir</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 77. A very hard, brittle, silvery-white <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a>, iridium is the second-densest element (after <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>) with a density of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004225600000000000♠</span>22.56&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> as defined by experimental X-ray crystallography. However at room temperature and standard atmospheric pressure, iridium has a density of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004226500000000000♠</span>22.65&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7001400000000000000♠</span>0.04&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> higher than osmium measured the same way. It is the most <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>-resistant metal, even at temperatures as high as 2000&#160;°C. Although only certain molten salts and <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> are corrosive to solid iridium, finely divided iridium dust is much more reactive and can be flammable.
</p><p>Iridium was discovered in 1803 among insoluble impurities in natural <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Smithson_Tennant" title="Smithson Tennant">Smithson Tennant</a>, the primary discoverer, named iridium for the Greek goddess <a href="/wiki/Iris_(mythology)" title="Iris (mythology)">Iris</a>, personification of the rainbow, because of the striking and diverse colors of its salts. Iridium is <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth&#39;s crust">one of the rarest elements</a> in <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)#Earth&#39;s_crust" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>, with annual production and consumption of only three <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>. <sup>191</sup>Ir and <sup>193</sup>Ir are the only two naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of iridium, as well as the only <a href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Stable isotope">stable isotopes</a>; the latter is the more abundant of the two.
</p><p>The most important iridium compounds in use are the salts and acids it forms with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, though iridium also forms a number of <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organometallic compound">organometallic compounds</a> used in industrial <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a>, and in research. Iridium metal is employed when high corrosion resistance at high temperatures is needed, as in high-performance <a href="/wiki/Spark_plug" title="Spark plug">spark plugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucibles</a> for recrystallization of semiconductors at high temperatures, and electrodes for the production of chlorine in the <a href="/wiki/Chloralkali_process" title="Chloralkali process">chloralkali process</a>. Iridium radioisotopes are used in some <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>.
</p><p>Iridium is found in meteorites in much higher abundance than in the Earth's crust.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker2002_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, the unusually high abundance of iridium in the clay layer at the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a> gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Alvarez_hypothesis" title="Alvarez hypothesis">Alvarez hypothesis</a> that the impact of a massive extraterrestrial object caused the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">extinction of dinosaurs and many other species 66&#160;million years ago</a>. Similarly, an iridium anomaly in core samples from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> suggested the <a href="/wiki/Eltanin_impact" title="Eltanin impact">Eltanin impact</a> of about 2.5 million years ago.
</p><p>It is thought that the total amount of iridium in the planet Earth is much higher than that observed in crustal rocks, but as with other platinum-group metals, the high density and <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Siderophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">tendency</a> of iridium to bond with iron caused most iridium to descend below the crust when the planet was young and still molten.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iridium2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A flattened drop of dark gray substance" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Iridium2.jpg/220px-Iridium2.jpg" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Iridium2.jpg/330px-Iridium2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Iridium2.jpg/440px-Iridium2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iridium2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One <a href="/wiki/Troy_ounce" class="mw-redirect" title="Troy ounce">troy ounce</a> (31.1035&#160;g) of arc-melted iridium</div></div></div>
<p>A member of the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, iridium is white, resembling <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, but with a slight yellowish cast. Because of its <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a>, brittleness, and very high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, solid iridium is difficult to machine, form, or work; thus <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder metallurgy</a> is commonly employed instead.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It is the only metal to maintain good mechanical properties in air at temperatures above 1,600&#160;°C (2,910&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It has the 10th highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_elements_by_boiling_point" class="mw-redirect" title="List of elements by boiling point">boiling point among all elements</a> and becomes a <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> at temperatures below 0.14&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium's <a href="/wiki/Modulus_of_elasticity" class="mw-redirect" title="Modulus of elasticity">modulus of elasticity</a> is the second-highest among the metals, only being surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This, together with a high <a href="/wiki/Shear_modulus" title="Shear modulus">shear modulus</a> and a very low figure for <a href="/wiki/Poisson%27s_ratio" title="Poisson&#39;s ratio">Poisson's ratio</a> (the relationship of longitudinal to lateral <a href="/wiki/Strain_(chemistry)" title="Strain (chemistry)">strain</a>), indicate the high degree of <a href="/wiki/Stiffness" title="Stiffness">stiffness</a> and resistance to deformation that have rendered its fabrication into useful components a matter of great difficulty. Despite these limitations and iridium's high cost, a number of applications have developed where mechanical strength is an essential factor in some of the extremely severe conditions encountered in modern technology.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The measured <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of iridium is only slightly lower (by about 0.12%) than that of osmium, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_elements_by_density" class="mw-redirect" title="List of elements by density">densest element</a> known.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Some ambiguity occurred regarding which of the two elements was denser, due to the small size of the difference in density and difficulties in measuring it accurately,<sup id="cite_ref-crc_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crc-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> but, with increased accuracy in factors used for calculating density <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallographic</a> data yielded densities of 22.56&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> for iridium and 22.59&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> for osmium.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Iridium is the most corrosion-resistant metal known:<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> it is not attacked by almost any <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, molten metals, or silicates at high temperatures. It can, however, be attacked by some molten <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_cyanide" title="Sodium cyanide">sodium cyanide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">potassium cyanide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-perry_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perry-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> at higher temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-lagowski_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lagowski-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Iridium_compounds" title="Category:Iridium compounds">Category:Iridium compounds</a></div>
<p>Iridium forms compounds in <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> between −3 and +9; the most common oxidation states are +3 and +4.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Well-characterized examples of the high +6 oxidation state are rare, but include <a href="/wiki/Iridium(VI)_fluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium(VI) fluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">IrF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a> and two mixed oxides <span class="chemf nowrap">Sr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>MgIrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Sr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CaIrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In addition, it was reported in 2009 that <a href="/wiki/Iridium(VIII)_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium(VIII) oxide">iridium(VIII) oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">IrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) was prepared under matrix isolation conditions (6 K in Ar) by UV irradiation of an iridium-peroxo complex. This species, however, is not expected to be stable as a bulk solid at higher temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The highest oxidation state (+9), which is also the highest recorded for <i>any</i> element, is only known in one cation, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>; it is only known as gas-phase species and is not known to form any <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IrIX_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrIX-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iridium(IV)_oxide" title="Iridium(IV) oxide">Iridium dioxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, a blue black solid, is the only well-characterized oxide of iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Sesquioxide" title="Sesquioxide">sesquioxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, has been described as a blue-black powder which is oxidized to <span class="chemf nowrap">IrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> by <span class="chemf nowrap">HNO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-perry_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perry-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The corresponding disulfides, diselenides, sesquisulfides, and sesquiselenides are known, and <span class="chemf nowrap">IrS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> has also been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Iridium also forms iridates with oxidation states +4 and +5, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>IrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">KIrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, which can be prepared from the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_oxide" title="Potassium oxide">potassium oxide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_superoxide" title="Potassium superoxide">potassium superoxide</a> with iridium at high temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although no <a href="/wiki/Binary_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary compound">binary</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a> of iridium, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>x</i></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>y</i></sub></span></span> are known, complexes are known that contain <span class="chemf nowrap">IrH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">IrH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, where iridium has the +1 and +3 oxidation states, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The ternary hydride <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">11</sub></span></span> is believed to contain both the <span class="chemf nowrap">IrH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> and the 18-electron <span class="chemf nowrap">IrH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>No monohalides or dihalides are known, whereas trihalides, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, are known for all of the halogens.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> For oxidation states +4 and above, only the <a href="/wiki/Iridium(IV)_fluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium(IV) fluoride">tetrafluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridium(V)_fluoride" title="Iridium(V) fluoride">pentafluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iridium_hexafluoride" title="Iridium hexafluoride">hexafluoride</a> are known.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Iridium hexafluoride, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, is a volatile and highly reactive yellow solid, composed of octahedral molecules. It decomposes in water and is reduced to <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Iridium_tetrafluoride" title="Iridium tetrafluoride">IrF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></a></span>, a crystalline solid, by iridium black.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Iridium pentafluoride has similar properties but it is actually a <a href="/wiki/Tetramer" title="Tetramer">tetramer</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">20</sub></span></span>, formed by four corner-sharing octahedra.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Iridium metal dissolves in molten alkali-metal cyanides to produce the <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> (hexacyanoiridate) ion.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png" class="image"><img alt="Skeletal formula of a chemical compound with iridium atom in its center bonded to two P-PH3 groups, to a chlorine atom and to a C-O group." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png/220px-Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png/330px-Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png/440px-Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="595" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vaska%27s-complex-2D.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vaska%27s_complex" title="Vaska&#39;s complex">Vaska's complex</a></div></div></div>
<p>Hexachloroiridic(IV) acid, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, and its ammonium salt are the most important iridium compounds from an industrial perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> They are involved in the purification of iridium and used as precursors for most other iridium compounds, as well as in the preparation of <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> coatings. The <span class="chemf nowrap">IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> ion has an intense dark brown color, and can be readily reduced to the lighter-colored <span class="chemf nowrap">IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> and vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iridium(III)_chloride" title="Iridium(III) chloride">Iridium trichloride</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, which can be obtained in anhydrous form from direct oxidation of iridium powder by <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> at 650&#160;°C,<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> or in hydrated form by dissolving <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, is often used as a starting material for the synthesis of other Ir(III) compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Another compound used as a starting material is ammonium hexachloroiridate(III), <span class="chemf nowrap">(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>. Iridium(III) complexes are <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Low-spin" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-spin">low-spin</a>) and generally have an <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral molecular geometry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Organoiridium_compound" title="Organoiridium compound">Organoiridium compounds</a> contain iridium–<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> bonds where the metal is usually in lower oxidation states. For example, oxidation state zero is found in <a href="/wiki/Tetrairidium_dodecacarbonyl" title="Tetrairidium dodecacarbonyl">tetrairidium dodecacarbonyl</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span></span>, which is the most common and stable binary <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">carbonyl</a> of iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In this compound, each of the iridium atoms is bonded to the other three, forming a tetrahedral cluster. Some organometallic Ir(I) compounds are notable enough to be named after their discoverers. One is <a href="/wiki/Vaska%27s_complex" title="Vaska&#39;s complex">Vaska's complex</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">IrCl(CO)[P(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, which has the unusual property of binding to the <a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_molecule" class="mw-redirect" title="Dioxygen molecule">dioxygen molecule</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Another one is <a href="/wiki/Crabtree%27s_catalyst" title="Crabtree&#39;s catalyst">Crabtree's catalyst</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Homogeneous catalyst">homogeneous catalyst</a> for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> These compounds are both <a href="/wiki/Square_planar" class="mw-redirect" title="Square planar">square planar</a>, d<sup>8</sup> complexes, with a total of 16 <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>, which accounts for their reactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An iridium-based organic <a href="/wiki/LED" class="mw-redirect" title="LED">LED</a> material has been documented, and found to be much brighter than <a href="/wiki/9,10-Diphenylanthracene" title="9,10-Diphenylanthracene">DPA</a> or <a href="/wiki/Poly(p-phenylene_vinylene)" title="Poly(p-phenylene vinylene)">PPV</a>, so could be the basis for flexible OLED lighting in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iridium" title="Isotopes of iridium">Isotopes of iridium</a></div>
<p>Iridium has two naturally occurring, stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <sup>191</sup>Ir and <sup>193</sup>Ir, with <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundances</a> of 37.3% and 62.7%, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> At least 34 <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have also been synthesized, ranging in <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> from 164 to 199. <a href="/wiki/Iridium-192" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium-192"><sup>192</sup>Ir</a>, which falls between the two stable isotopes, is the most stable radioisotope, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 73.827&#160;days, and finds application in <a href="/wiki/Brachytherapy" title="Brachytherapy">brachytherapy</a><sup id="cite_ref-mager_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mager-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and in industrial <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a>, particularly for nondestructive testing of welds in steel in the oil and gas industries; iridium-192 sources have been involved in a number of radiological accidents. Three other isotopes have half-lives of at least a day—<sup>188</sup>Ir, <sup>189</sup>Ir, and <sup>190</sup>Ir.<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Isotopes with masses below 191 decay by some combination of <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay#β+_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>+</sup> decay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">α decay</a>, and (rare) <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a>, with the exceptions of <sup>189</sup>Ir, which decays by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>. Synthetic isotopes heavier than 191 decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay#β+_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup> decay</a>, although <sup>192</sup>Ir also has a minor electron capture decay path.<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> All known isotopes of iridium were discovered between 1934 and 2001; the most recent is <sup>171</sup>Ir.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At least 32 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable isomers</a> have been characterized, ranging in mass number from 164 to 197. The most stable of these is <sup>192m2</sup>Ir, which decays by <a href="/wiki/Isomeric_transition" class="mw-redirect" title="Isomeric transition">isomeric transition</a> with a half-life of 241&#160;years,<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> making it more stable than any of iridium's synthetic isotopes in their ground states. The least stable isomer is <sup>190m3</sup>Ir with a half-life of only 2&#160;µs.<sup id="cite_ref-nubase_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The isotope <sup>191</sup>Ir was the first one of any element to be shown to present a <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_effect" title="Mössbauer effect">Mössbauer effect</a>. This renders it useful for <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_spectroscopy" title="Mössbauer spectroscopy">Mössbauer spectroscopy</a> for research in physics, chemistry, biochemistry, metallurgy, and mineralogy.<sup id="cite_ref-ir-191_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ir-191-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Platinum_group">Platinum group</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Platinum group">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of part of a black vase with brown picture on it: A woman with wings on her back hold an arrow with right hand and gives a jar to a man. A small deer is standing in front of the woman." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg/220px-Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg" width="220" height="310" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg/330px-Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg/440px-Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1850" data-file-height="2610" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Winged_goddess_Cdm_Paris_392.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Greek goddess <a href="/wiki/Iris_(mythology)" title="Iris (mythology)">Iris</a>, after whom iridium was named.</div></div></div>
<p>The discovery of iridium is intertwined with that of platinum and the other metals of the platinum group. <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">Native</a> platinum used by ancient Ethiopians<sup id="cite_ref-Egypt_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egypt-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and by South American cultures<sup id="cite_ref-preCol_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-preCol-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> always contained a small amount of the other platinum group metals, including iridium. Platinum reached Europe as <i>platina</i> ("silverette"), found in the 17th century by the Spanish conquerors in a region today known as the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Choc%C3%B3" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Chocó">department of Chocó</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The discovery that this metal was not an alloy of known elements, but instead a distinct new element, did not occur until 1748.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chemists who studied platinum dissolved it in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a> (a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acids</a>) to create soluble salts. They always observed a small amount of a dark, insoluble residue.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Proust" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Louis Proust">Joseph Louis Proust</a> thought that the residue was <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The French chemists <a href="/wiki/Victor_Collet-Descotils" class="mw-redirect" title="Victor Collet-Descotils">Victor Collet-Descotils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Fran%C3%A7ois,_comte_de_Fourcroy" title="Antoine François, comte de Fourcroy">Antoine François, comte de Fourcroy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin" title="Louis Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis Nicolas Vauquelin</a> also observed the black residue in 1803, but did not obtain enough for further experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1803, British scientist <a href="/wiki/Smithson_Tennant" title="Smithson Tennant">Smithson Tennant</a> (1761–1815) analyzed the insoluble residue and concluded that it must contain a new metal. Vauquelin treated the powder alternately with alkali and acids<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and obtained a volatile new oxide, which he believed to be of this new metal—which he named <i>ptene</i>, from the Greek word <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">πτηνός</span> <i>ptēnós</i>, "winged".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-griffith_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffith-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Tennant, who had the advantage of a much greater amount of residue, continued his research and identified the two previously undiscovered elements in the black residue, iridium and osmium.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He obtained dark red crystals (probably of <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>]·<i>n</i><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) by a sequence of reactions with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-griffith_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffith-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He named iridium after Iris (<span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Ἶρις</span>), the Greek winged goddess of the rainbow and the messenger of the Olympian gods, because many of the <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> he obtained were strongly colored.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weeks_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weeks-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Discovery of the new elements was documented in a letter to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> on June 21, 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metalworking_and_applications">Metalworking and applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Metalworking and applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>British scientist <a href="/wiki/John_George_Children" title="John George Children">John George Children</a> was the first to melt a sample of iridium in 1813 with the aid of "the greatest galvanic battery that has ever been constructed" (at that time).<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The first to obtain high-purity iridium was <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hare_(chemist)" title="Robert Hare (chemist)">Robert Hare</a> in 1842. He found it had a density of around 21.8&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> and noted the metal is nearly immalleable and very hard. The first melting in appreciable quantity was done by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Sainte-Claire_Deville" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Sainte-Claire Deville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Henri_Debray" title="Jules Henri Debray">Jules Henri Debray</a> in 1860. They required burning more than 300&#160;liters of pure <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> gas for each kilogram of iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These extreme difficulties in melting the metal limited the possibilities for handling iridium. <a href="/wiki/John_Isaac_Hawkins" title="John Isaac Hawkins">John Isaac Hawkins</a> was looking to obtain a fine and hard point for fountain pen nibs, and in 1834 managed to create an iridium-pointed gold pen. In 1880, <a href="/wiki/John_Holland_(pen_maker)" title="John Holland (pen maker)">John Holland</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Lofland_Dudley" title="William Lofland Dudley">William Lofland Dudley</a> were able to melt iridium by adding <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and patented the process in the United States; British company <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Matthey" title="Johnson Matthey">Johnson Matthey</a> later stated they had been using a similar process since 1837 and had already presented fused iridium at a number of <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World&#39;s fair">World Fairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The first use of an alloy of iridium with ruthenium in <a href="/wiki/Thermocouple" title="Thermocouple">thermocouples</a> was made by Otto Feussner in 1933. These allowed for the measurement of high temperatures in air up to 2000&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Munich, Germany in 1957 <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_M%C3%B6ssbauer" title="Rudolf Mössbauer">Rudolf Mössbauer</a>, in what has been called one of the "landmark experiments in twentieth-century physics",<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> discovered the resonant and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Recoil" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic Recoil">recoil</a>-free emission and absorption of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> by atoms in a solid metal sample containing only <sup>191</sup>Ir.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> This phenomenon, known as the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_effect" title="Mössbauer effect">Mössbauer effect</a> (which has since been observed for other nuclei, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron-57" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron-57"><sup>57</sup>Fe</a>), and developed as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_spectroscopy" title="Mössbauer spectroscopy">Mössbauer spectroscopy</a>, has made important contributions to research in physics, chemistry, biochemistry, metallurgy, and mineralogy.<sup id="cite_ref-ir-191_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ir-191-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Mössbauer received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in 1961, at the age 32, just three years after he published his discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In 1986 <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_M%C3%B6ssbauer" title="Rudolf Mössbauer">Rudolf Mössbauer</a> was honored for his achievements with the Albert Einstein Medal and the Elliot Cresson Medal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elemental_abundances.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/350px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png" width="350" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/525px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/700px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elemental_abundances.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iridium is one of the least abundant elements in Earth's crust.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large black egg-shaped boulder of porous structure standing on its top, tilted" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg/220px-Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg/330px-Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg/440px-Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Willamette_Meteorite_AMNH.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Willamette_Meteorite" title="Willamette Meteorite">Willamette Meteorite</a>, the sixth-largest meteorite found in the world, has 4.7&#160;ppm iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Iridium is one of the nine least abundant stable elements in Earth's crust, having an average mass fraction of 0.001&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> in crustal rock; <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> is 40 times more abundant, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> is 10 times more abundant, and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> are 80 times more abundant.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">Tellurium</a> is about as abundant as iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-greenwood_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to its low abundance in crustal rock, iridium is relatively common in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>, with concentrations of 0.5&#160;ppm or more.<sup id="cite_ref-argonne_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argonne-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The overall concentration of iridium on Earth is thought to be much higher than what is observed in crustal rocks, but because of the density and <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification" title="Goldschmidt classification">siderophilic</a> ("iron-loving") character of iridium, it descended below the crust and into <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">Earth's core</a> when the planet was still molten.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium is found in nature as an uncombined element or in natural <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>; especially the iridium–osmium alloys, <a href="/wiki/Osmiridium" title="Osmiridium">osmiridium</a> (osmium-rich), and <a href="/wiki/Iridosmium" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridosmium">iridosmium</a> (iridium-rich).<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> deposits, the platinum group metals occur as <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> (i.e. (Pt,Pd)S), <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">tellurides</a> (i.e. PtBiTe), <a href="/wiki/Antimonide" title="Antimonide">antimonides</a> (PdSb), and <a href="/wiki/Arsenide" title="Arsenide">arsenides</a> (i.e. <span class="chemf nowrap">PtAs<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). In all of these compounds, platinum is exchanged by a small amount of iridium and osmium. As with all of the platinum group metals, iridium can be found naturally in alloys with raw nickel or <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">raw copper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A number of iridium-dominant minerals, with iridium as the species-forming element, are known. They are exceedingly rare and often represent the iridium analogues of the above-given ones. The examples are irarsite and cuproiridsite, to mention some.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within Earth's crust, iridium is found at highest concentrations in three types of geologic structure: igneous deposits (crustal intrusions from below), impact craters, and deposits reworked from one of the former structures. The largest known primary reserves are in the <a href="/wiki/Bushveld_igneous_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Bushveld igneous complex">Bushveld igneous complex</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> though the large copper–nickel deposits near <a href="/wiki/Norilsk#Norilsk-Talnakh_nickel_deposits" title="Norilsk">Norilsk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Basin" title="Sudbury Basin">Sudbury Basin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> are also significant sources of iridium. Smaller reserves are found in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Iridium is also found in secondary deposits, combined with platinum and other platinum group metals in <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvial</a> deposits. The alluvial deposits used by <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> people in the <a href="/wiki/Choc%C3%B3_Department" title="Chocó Department">Chocó Department</a> of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> are still a source for platinum-group metals.  As of 2003, world reserves have not been estimated.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Cretaceous.E2.80.93Paleogene_boundary_presence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cretaceous–Paleogene_boundary_presence">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary presence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary presence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:K-T_boundary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A cliff with pronounced layered structure: yellow, gray, white, gray. A red arrow points between the yellow and gray layers." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/K-T_boundary.jpg/220px-K-T_boundary.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/K-T_boundary.jpg/330px-K-T_boundary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/K-T_boundary.jpg/440px-K-T_boundary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:K-T_boundary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red arrow points to the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a></div>
<p>The [Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary] of 66 million years ago, marking the temporal border between the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> periods of <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">geological time</a>, was identified by a thin <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">stratum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iridium_anomaly" title="Iridium anomaly">iridium-rich clay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alvarez_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A team led by <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Alvarez</a> proposed in 1980 an extraterrestrial origin for this iridium, attributing it to an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> impact.<sup id="cite_ref-Alvarez_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Their theory, known as the <a href="/wiki/Alvarez_hypothesis" title="Alvarez hypothesis">Alvarez hypothesis</a>, is now widely accepted to explain the extinction of the non-avian <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>. A large buried impact crater structure with an estimated age of about 66 million years was later identified under what is now the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">Chicxulub crater</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Dewey M. McLean and others argue that the iridium may have been of <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> origin instead, because <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s core is rich in iridium, and active volcanoes such as <a href="/wiki/Piton_de_la_Fournaise" title="Piton de la Fournaise">Piton de la Fournaise</a>, in the island of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="Réunion">Réunion</a>, are still releasing iridium.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float:right; margin-left:0.5em">
