<p>Iridium is also obtained commercially as a by-product from <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> mining and processing. During <a href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques#Electrorefining" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper extraction techniques">electrorefining of copper</a> and nickel, noble metals such as silver, gold and the <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group_metal" class="mw-redirect" title="Platinum group metal">platinum group metals</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> settle to the bottom of the cell as <i>anode mud</i>, which forms the starting point for their extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> To separate the metals, they must first be brought into solution. Several separation methods are available depending on the nature of the mixture; two representative methods are fusion with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_peroxide" title="Sodium peroxide">sodium peroxide</a> followed by dissolution in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, and dissolution in a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-pt_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-pt-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the mixture is dissolved, iridium is separated from the other platinum group metals by precipitating <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hexachloroiridate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammonium hexachloroiridate">ammonium hexachloroiridate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>) or by extracting <span class="chemf nowrap">IrCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> with organic amines.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The first method is similar to the procedure Tennant and Wollaston used for their separation. The second method can be planned as continuous <a href="/wiki/Liquid%E2%80%93liquid_extraction" title="Liquid–liquid extraction">liquid–liquid extraction</a> and is therefore more suitable for industrial scale production. In either case, the product is reduced using hydrogen, yielding the metal as a powder or <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_sponge" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal sponge">sponge</a></i> that can be treated using <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder metallurgy</a> techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium prices have fluctuated over a considerable range. With a relatively small volume in the world market (compared to other industrial metals like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>), the iridium price reacts strongly to instabilities in production, demand, <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a>, hoarding, and politics in the producing countries.
As a substance with rare properties, its price has been particularly influenced by changes in modern technology:
The gradual decrease between 2001 and 2003 has been related to an oversupply of Ir crucibles used for industrial growth of large single crystals.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
Likewise the prices above 1000&#8239;USD/oz between 2010 and 2014 have been explained with the installation of production facilities for single crystal <a href="/wiki/Corundum#Synthetic_corundum" title="Corundum">sapphire</a> used in <a href="/wiki/LED" class="mw-redirect" title="LED">LED</a> backlights for TVs.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The demand for iridium surged from 2.5 tonnes in 2009 to 10.4 tonnes in 2010, mostly because of electronics-related applications that saw a rise from 0.2 to 6 tonnes – iridium crucibles are commonly used for growing large high-quality single crystals, demand for which has increased sharply. This increase in iridium consumption is predicted to saturate due to accumulating stocks of crucibles, as happened earlier in the 2000s. Other major applications include spark plugs that consumed 0.78 tonnes of iridium in 2007, electrodes for the <a href="/wiki/Chloralkali_process" title="Chloralkali process">chloralkali process</a> (1.1 t in 2007) and chemical catalysts (0.75 t in 2007).<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-platinum2008_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-platinum2008-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_and_medical">Industrial and medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Industrial and medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ir(mppy)3.png" class="image"><img alt="Skeletal formula of a chemical compound with iridium atom in its center, bonded to 6 benzol rings. The rings are pairwise connected to each other." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ir%28mppy%293.png/170px-Ir%28mppy%293.png" width="170" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ir%28mppy%293.png/255px-Ir%28mppy%293.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ir%28mppy%293.png/340px-Ir%28mppy%293.png 2x" data-file-width="489" data-file-height="519" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ir(mppy)3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molecular structure of <span class="chemf nowrap">Ir(mppy)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></div></div></div>
<p>The high melting point, hardness and corrosion resistance of iridium and its alloys determine most of its applications. Iridium (or sometimes platinum alloys or osmium) and mostly iridium alloys have a low wear and are used, for example, for multi-pored <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(polymers)" title="Spinneret (polymers)">spinnerets</a>, through which a plastic polymer melt is extruded to form fibers, such as <a href="/wiki/Rayon" title="Rayon">rayon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Osmium–iridium is used for <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a> bearings and for balances.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Their resistance to arc erosion makes iridium alloys ideal for electrical contacts for <a href="/wiki/Spark_plug" title="Spark plug">spark plugs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Handley_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handley-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and iridium-based spark plugs are particularly used in aviation.
</p><p>Pure iridium is extremely brittle, to the point of being hard to weld because the heat-affected zone cracks, but it can be made more ductile by addition of small quantities of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> (0.2% of each apparently works well)<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Corrosion and heat resistance makes iridium an important alloying agent. Certain long-life aircraft engine parts are made of an iridium alloy, and an iridium–<a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> alloy is used for deep-water pipes because of its corrosion resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Iridium is also used as a hardening agent in platinum alloys. The <a href="/wiki/Vickers_hardness" class="mw-redirect" title="Vickers hardness">Vickers hardness</a> of pure platinum is 56&#160;HV, whereas platinum with 50% of iridium can reach over 500&#160;HV.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Devices that must withstand extremely high temperatures are often made from iridium. For example, high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucibles</a> made of iridium are used in the <a href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">Czochralski process</a> to produce oxide single-crystals (such as <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphires</a>) for use in computer memory devices and in solid state lasers.<sup id="cite_ref-Handley_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handley-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The crystals, such as <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium_gallium_garnet" title="Gadolinium gallium garnet">gadolinium gallium garnet</a> and yttrium gallium garnet, are grown by melting pre-sintered charges of mixed oxides under oxidizing conditions at temperatures up to 2100&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cativa_process" title="Cativa process">Cativa process</a> for <a href="/wiki/Carbonylation" title="Carbonylation">carbonylation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-acetic_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-acetic-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The radioisotope <a href="/wiki/Iridium-192" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium-192">iridium-192</a> is one of the two most important sources of energy for use in industrial <a href="/wiki/Industrial_radiography#Radioisotope_sources" title="Industrial radiography">γ-radiography</a> for <a href="/wiki/Non-destructive_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-destructive testing">non-destructive testing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hellier_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellier-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, <sup>192</sup>Ir is used as a source of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma radiation">gamma radiation</a> for the treatment of cancer using <a href="/wiki/Brachytherapy" title="Brachytherapy">brachytherapy</a>, a form of radiotherapy where a sealed radioactive source is placed inside or next to the area requiring treatment. Specific treatments include high-dose-rate prostate brachytherapy, bilary duct brachytherapy, and intracavitary cervix brachytherapy.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium is a good catalyst for the decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> (into hot nitrogen and ammonia), and this is used in practice in low-thrust rocket engines; there are more details in the <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant rocket</a> article.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific">Scientific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Scientific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="NIST Library US Prototype meter bar" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg/220px-Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg/330px-Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg/440px-Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4232" data-file-height="2781" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Platinum-Iridium_meter_bar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_Metre" class="mw-redirect" title="International Prototype Metre">International Prototype Metre</a> bar</div></div></div>
<p>An alloy of 90% platinum and 10% iridium was used in 1889 to construct the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_Metre" class="mw-redirect" title="International Prototype Metre">International Prototype Metre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kilogram#International_prototype_kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> mass, kept by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_International_des_Poids_et_Mesures" class="mw-redirect" title="Bureau International des Poids et Mesures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> near <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The meter bar was replaced as the definition of the fundamental unit of length in 1960 by a line in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_spectrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic spectrum">atomic spectrum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Krypton#Metric_role" title="Krypton">krypton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meter_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meter-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> but the kilogram prototype is still the international standard of mass.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium has been used in the <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> of unmanned spacecraft such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini-Huygens" class="mw-redirect" title="Cassini-Huygens">Cassini</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>. Iridium was chosen to encapsulate the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a> fuel in the generator because it can withstand the operating temperatures of up to 2000&#160;°C and for its great strength.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another use concerns X-ray optics, especially X-ray telescopes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ziegler_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The mirrors of the <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> are coated with a layer of iridium 60&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nanometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometer">nm</a> thick. Iridium proved to be the best choice for reflecting X-rays after nickel, gold, and platinum were also tested. The iridium layer, which had to be smooth to within a few atoms, was applied by depositing iridium vapor under <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">high vacuum</a> on a base layer of <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium is used in <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> for the production of <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiprotons</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>. Antiprotons are made by shooting a high-intensity proton beam at a <i>conversion target</i>, which needs to be made from a very high density material. Although <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> may be used instead, iridium has the advantage of better stability under the <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock waves</a> induced by the temperature rise due to the incident beam.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C-HactnBergGrah.png" class="image"><img alt="Skeletal formula presentation of a chemical transformation. The initial compounds have a C5H5 ring on their top and an iridium atom in the center, which is bonded to two hydrogen atoms and a P-PH3 group or to two C-O groups. Reaction with alkane under UV light alters those groups." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/C-HactnBergGrah.png/440px-C-HactnBergGrah.png" width="440" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/C-HactnBergGrah.png/660px-C-HactnBergGrah.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/C-HactnBergGrah.png/880px-C-HactnBergGrah.png 2x" data-file-width="2056" data-file-height="475" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C-HactnBergGrah.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oxidative addition to hydrocarbons in <a href="/wiki/Organoiridium_chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Organoiridium chemistry">organoiridium chemistry</a><sup id="cite_ref-RGB_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGB-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WAGG_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAGG-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/C-H_bond_activation" class="mw-redirect" title="C-H bond activation">Carbon–hydrogen bond activation</a> (C–H activation) is an area of research on reactions that cleave <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93hydrogen_bond" title="Carbon–hydrogen bond">carbon–hydrogen bonds</a>, which were traditionally regarded as unreactive. The first reported successes at activating C–H bonds in <a href="/wiki/Saturated_hydrocarbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saturated hydrocarbon">saturated hydrocarbons</a>, published in 1982, used organometallic iridium complexes that undergo an <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_addition" title="Oxidative addition">oxidative addition</a> with the hydrocarbon.<sup id="cite_ref-RGB_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGB-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WAGG_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAGG-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium complexes are being investigated as catalysts for <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_hydrogenation" title="Asymmetric hydrogenation">asymmetric hydrogenation</a>. These catalysts have been used in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a> and able to hydrogenate certain difficult substrates, such as unfunctionalized alkenes, enantioselectively (generating only one of the two possible <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Iridium forms a variety of <a href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex (chemistry)">complexes</a> of fundamental interest in triplet harvesting.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical">Historical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Historical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG/220px-Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG/330px-Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG/440px-Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gama_Supreme_Flat_Top_ebonite_eyedropper_fountain_pen_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">Fountain pen</a> nib labeled <i>Iridium Point</i></div></div></div>
<p>Iridium–osmium alloys were used in <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a> <a href="/wiki/Nib_(pen)#Nib_tipping" title="Nib (pen)">nib tips</a>. The first major use of iridium was in 1834 in nibs mounted on gold.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Since 1944, the famous <a href="/wiki/Parker_51" title="Parker 51">Parker 51</a> fountain pen was fitted with a nib tipped by a ruthenium and iridium alloy (with 3.8% iridium). The tip material in modern fountain pens is still conventionally called "iridium", although there is seldom any iridium in it; other metals such as ruthenium, osmium and tungsten have taken its place.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An iridium–platinum alloy was used for the <a href="/wiki/Touch_hole" title="Touch hole">touch holes</a> or vent pieces of <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>. According to a report of the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1867)" title="Exposition Universelle (1867)">Paris Exhibition of 1867</a>, one of the pieces being exhibited by <a href="/wiki/Johnson_and_Matthey" class="mw-redirect" title="Johnson and Matthey">Johnson and Matthey</a> "has been used in a Withworth gun for more than 3000 rounds, and scarcely shows signs of wear yet. Those who know the constant trouble and expense which are occasioned by the wearing of the vent-pieces of cannon when in active service, will appreciate this important adaptation".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pigment <i>iridium black</i>, which consists of very finely divided iridium, is used for painting <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> an intense black; it was said that "all other porcelain black colors appear grey by the side of it".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Iridium in bulk metallic form is not biologically important or hazardous to health due to its lack of reactivity with tissues; there are only about 20&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_notation" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per notation">parts per trillion</a> of iridium in human tissue.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Like most metals, finely divided iridium powder can be hazardous to handle, as it is an irritant and may ignite in air.<sup id="cite_ref-kirk-pt_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-pt-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
Very little is known about the toxicity of iridium compounds, because they are used in very small amounts, but soluble salts, such as the iridium halides, could be hazardous due to elements other than iridium or due to iridium itself.<sup id="cite_ref-mager_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mager-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, most iridium compounds are insoluble, which makes absorption into the body difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A radioisotope of iridium, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">192</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ir</span>, is dangerous, like other radioactive isotopes. The only reported injuries related to iridium concern accidental exposure to radiation from <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">192</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ir</span> used in <a href="/wiki/Brachytherapy" title="Brachytherapy">brachytherapy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mager_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mager-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> High-energy gamma radiation from <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">192</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ir</span> can increase the risk of cancer. External exposure can cause burns, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation poisoning">radiation poisoning</a>, and death. Ingestion of <sup>192</sup>Ir can burn the linings of the stomach and the intestines.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <sup>192</sup>Ir, <sup>192m</sup>Ir, and <sup>194m</sup>Ir tend to deposit in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, and can pose health hazards from both <a href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma radiation">gamma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta</a> radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-argonne_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-argonne-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Iridium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most common oxidation states of iridium are in bold. The right column lists one representative compound for each oxidation state.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Iridium</i> literally means "of rainbows".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The definition of the meter was changed again in 1983. The meter is currently defined as the distance traveled by light in a vacuum during a time interval of &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>299,792,458</sub></span> of a second.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
